Making Nabidh in Previously Prohibited Vessels
Ruling on permissibility of nabidh in specific vessels with proof of abrogation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on making nabidh in dubba', hantam, and mazaft vessels and explain the dalil of abrogation.

Core Idea
The vessel does not make anything halal or haram; only intoxicants are prohibited.

This lesson addresses a ruling from Al-Hidayah on using certain vessels that were initially prohibited for making nabidh. You will learn how the Prophet's later permission abrogated the earlier ban, and why the Hanafi school considers these vessels permissible for non-intoxicating drinks.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is making nabidh in dubba', hantam, and mazaft vessels. The ruling is permissibility (la ba'sa).
The passage states: 'There is no harm in making nabidh in dubba', hantam, and mazaft.'
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is a hadith where the Prophet said: 'Drink in every vessel, for the vessel does not make anything halal or haram, and do not drink intoxicants.'
This hadith is cited after mentioning the three vessels.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'ishrabu' indicates permissibility. The general phrase 'in every vessel' abrogates the earlier specific prohibition. The statement that the vessel does not affect halal or haram establishes that legal rulings depend on the contents, not the container.
The hadith explicitly says the vessel itself is neutral.
4
Consider the historical context
Initially, the Prophet prohibited these vessels to instill aversion to wine, as they were commonly used for wine. When the purpose was achieved, the prohibition was lifted.
This shows the prohibition was temporary and conditional.
5
Apply the usul principle of naskh
The later general permission abrogates the earlier specific prohibition. The Hanafi school accepts this naskh, making the vessels permissible for nabidh.
The principle: a later ruling can abrogate an earlier one if clearly contradictory.

Source Text

دباء ختم اور مزفت میں نبیذ بنانے کا حکم
قال ولا بأس بالانتباذ فى الدباء والحنتم والمزفت لقوله عليه السلام في حديث فيه طول بعد ذكر هذه الاوعية ف اشربوا في كل ظرف فان الظرف لا يحل شيئا ولا يحرمه ولا تشربوا المسكر وقال ذالك بعد عنـه فـكان نـ ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور کوئی حرج نہیں ہے کدو کے برتن میں اور سبز ٹھلیا میں اور زفت پھرے ہوئے برتن میں نبیذ بنانے سے نبی علیہ السلام کے فرمان کی وجہ سے اس حدیث میں جس میں طول ہے ان برتنوں کے ذکر کے بعد ( فرمایا ) کہ ہر برتن میں پیو اس لئے کہ برتن کسی چیز کو حلال نہیں کرتا اور نہ اس کو حرام کرتا اور مسکر نہ پہوا اور آپ نے یہ بات فرمائی تھی ان برتنوں سے ممانعت کے بعد تو یہ قول نہی کا ناسخ بن گیا۔ تشریح با کدو کا برتن جنتم سبز رنگ کی مٹی کی ٹھلیا، مرفت تارکول جیسی ایک چیز ہے جسے رفت کہتے ہیں جب کسی برتن پر اس کی پالش کر دی جائے تو اس برتن کو مزفت کہتے ہیں۔
بہر حال یہ تو برتنوں کے نام ہیں جیسے ہمارے یہاں ، گھڑا، کروا، رجنی ، رجنا ، مٹکا اور مطلب یہ ہے کہ لوگ ان برتنوں میں شراب بنایا کرتے تھے جب شراب کی حرمت نازل ہوئی تو جناب رسول اللہ صلى الله عليه وسلم نے ان برتنوں کے استعمال کی ممانعت فرمادی تھی تا کہ شراب کی نفرت دلوں میں پیوست ہو جائے پھر کچھ عرصہ بعد جب مقصد حاصل ہو گیا تو ان برتنوں کے استعمال کی اجازت مل گئی اور پہلا حکم منسوخ ہو گیا یہ حدیث مشکوۃ میں آپ کے سامنے گذر چکی ہے جہاں ان کی ممانعت ہے۔
Ruling on making nabidh in dubba', hantam, and mazaft.
He said: There is no harm in making nabidh in dubba' (gourd), hantam (earthenware), and mazaft (pitched vessel) because of his saying, peace be upon him, in a lengthy hadith after mentioning these vessels: 'Drink in every vessel, for the vessel does not make anything halal or haram, and do not drink intoxicants.' And he said that after the prohibition of these vessels, so this statement became an abrogator of the prohibition. Translation: Al-Quduri said: There is no harm in making nabidh in gourd vessels, green earthen jars, and vessels coated with pitch (zift) because of the Prophet's command in a lengthy hadith; after mentioning these vessels, he said: 'Drink in every vessel, for the vessel does not make anything halal or haram, and do not drink intoxicants.' And the Prophet said this after prohibiting these vessels, so this statement abrogated the prohibition. Explanation: Dubba' is a gourd vessel, hantam is a green earthen jar, mazaft is a vessel coated with pitch (zift). Anyway, these are names of vessels, like our pots, jars, etc. The point is that people used to make wine in these vessels. When the prohibition of wine was revealed, the Prophet forbade the use of these vessels to instill aversion to wine in hearts. Then after some time, when the purpose was achieved, permission was given to use these vessels, and the earlier ruling was abrogated. This hadith has passed before you in Mishkat where their prohibition is mentioned.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the commentary on Al-Hidayah. It addresses the permissibility of making nabidh (a type of date or grape infusion) in three specific vessels: dubba' (gourd), hantam (green earthenware), and mazaft (vessels coated with pitch). The key dalil is a hadith where the Prophet initially prohibited these vessels because they were commonly used for wine, but later abrogated that prohibition with a general permission: 'Drink in every vessel, for the vessel does not make anything halal or haram.' The Hanafi school holds that the vessel itself is not the cause of prohibition; rather, the intoxicant content is. Therefore, once the temporary ban (intended to create aversion to wine) was lifted, these vessels became permissible for nabidh. The passage clarifies that the specific names mentioned (dubba', hantam, mazaft) correspond to various types of common vessels, and the ruling is that they are now allowed for making non-intoxicating drinks.

Key Terms
نبیذ (nabidh): A drink made by soaking dates, raisins, or other fruits in water; often considered non-intoxicating if not fermented, but if fermented becomes intoxicating.
دباء (dubba'): A vessel made from a dried gourd; used for storing liquids.
حنتم (hantam): Green earthenware jars; also among the initially prohibited vessels.
مزفت (mazaft): A vessel coated with pitch (zift), a tar-like substance used for sealing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the basis for the permissibility of making nabidh in dubba', hantam, and mazaft vessels?
A) These vessels are naturally pure and never cause intoxication.
B) The Prophet initially prohibited them but later abrogated that prohibition by allowing drinking in any vessel.
C) They are only allowed for nabidh but not for water.
D) The Hanafi school considers them permissible only if washed three times.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of using vessels for nabidh hinges on the content, not the container; the Prophet's later general permission abrogated the specific vessel prohibition.

Wine Turning into Vinegar: Hanafi vs Shafi'i Ruling
Ruling on wine turning into vinegar and dispute with Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wine turning into vinegar, explain the Shafi'i position, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf between the two schools.

Core Idea
Istihalah transforms wine into a pure substance; thus vinegar is halal regardless of method.

This lesson explores a key difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding wine (khamr) that turns into vinegar. The Hanafi position, based on the principle of istihalah (chemical transformation), holds that vinegar from wine is halal and the process is permissible. Imam al-Shafi'i, however, restricts the permissibility based on how the transformation occurs. By understanding both positions and their proofs, you will appreciate how different usul al-fiqh principles lead to divergent rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling from the text: wine that becomes vinegar is halal, whether by itself or by adding something, and the act of making it is not makruh.
This is the established position from Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Identify the opposing (Shafi'i) view
State Imam al-Shafi'i's position: making vinegar from wine is makruh; if an additive is used, the vinegar is haram (one qawl); if spontaneous, there are two opinions.
Note the nuance: Shafi'i distinguishes based on the method of transformation.
3
Compare the proofs (dalil) for each
Hanafi proof: hadith 'What an excellent condiment is vinegar' and principle of istihalah (transformation changes ruling). Shafi'i proof: concern over promoting a prohibited substance and ritual impurity if an additive is involved.
The key difference is how each school views the effect of istihalah on the original prohibition.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school argues that istihalah fundamentally changes the essence of the substance, removing all traces of the original prohibition. The addition of a pure substance does not prevent this transformation.
This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on change of essence (taghayyur al-'ayn) as a cause for purification.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: For Hanafis, all vinegar from wine is halal and its production is allowed. For Shafi'is, only spontaneously produced vinegar may be considered, and that with ikhtilaf; vinegar from additives is definitively haram.
This affects everyday situations like using vinegar in food or selling such vinegar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شراب خود بخود دسر کہ بن جائے یا بنائی جائے تو حلال ہے
وقال واذا تخللت الخمر حلت سواء صارت خلا بنفسها اوبشئى يطرح فيها ولا يكره تخليلها رجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور جب خمر سر کہ بن گئی تو حلال ہو گئی خواہ وہ خود بخود دسر کہ ہو گئی ہو یا کسی ایسی چیز سے جو اس میں ڈالی گئی ہو اور اس کا سرکہ بنا نا مکروہ نہیں ہے۔ تشریح شراب کا سرکہ خود بنے یا بنایا جائے اب وہ حلال ہو جائے گی اور اس کا سرکہ بنانا مکر وہ بھی نہیں ہے۔
سرکہ بنانے میں امام شافعی کا نقطہ نظر
وقال الشافعي يكره التخليل ولا يحل الخل الحاصل به ان كان التخليل بالقاء شئى فيه قولا واحدا وان كان اء شـ ى ليـه فـلـه فـي الـخـل الـحــاصــــل بــــــــه قـــولان ترجمہ اور شافعی نے فرمایا کہ سرکہ بنانا ( خمر کا مکروہ ہے اور سر کہ جو خمر سے حاصل ہوا حلال نہیں ہوگا اگر سرکہ بنانا اس میں کوئی چیز ڈالنے کے ساتھ ہو ( اس صورت میں یہ ایک ہی قول ہے اور اگر اس میں کوئی چیز ڈالے بغیر ہو تو ان کے اس سرکہ کے بارے میں جو اس سے حاصل ہوا دو قول ہیں ۔ تشریح امام شافعی سرکہ بنانے کو مطلق مکروہ قرار دیتے ہیں اب رہا حاصل شدہ سرکہ تو اس میں تفصیل ہے (۱) اگر خمر میں کوئی چیز ڈال کر سرکہ بنایا گیا ہے تو امام شافعی کا اس میں صرف ایک ہی قول ہے کہ یہ سرکہ حلال نہیں ہے (۲) اور اگر بغیر کچھ ڈالے ہوئے سرکہ بنا ہو تو اس میں دو قول ہیں۔
Wine that becomes vinegar on its own or is made into vinegar is halal.
He said: And when wine turns into vinegar, it becomes halal, whether it became vinegar by itself or by something thrown into it, and making it into vinegar is not makruh. Translation: Al-Quduri said: And when khamr becomes vinegar, it becomes halal, whether it became vinegar by itself or by something that is thrown into it, and its being made into vinegar is not makruh. Explanation: Wine's vinegar whether it becomes by itself or is made, it becomes halal, and making it into vinegar is also not makruh.
Imam Shafi'i's view on making vinegar:
And al-Shafi'i said: Making vinegar (of wine) is makruh, and the vinegar obtained from it is not halal if the vinegar was made by throwing something into it, according to one definite opinion. And if it is without throwing anything, then regarding the vinegar obtained he has two opinions. Translation: And al-Shafi'i said that making vinegar (of khamr) is makruh, and the vinegar obtained from khamr will not be halal if the vinegar-making is by throwing something into it (in this case, there is one opinion), and if it is without throwing anything, then he has two opinions regarding the vinegar obtained. Explanation: Imam Shafi'i considers making vinegar absolutely makruh. As for the vinegar obtained, there is detail: (1) If vinegar was made by throwing something into the khamr, then Imam Shafi'i has only one opinion: that this vinegar is not halal. (2) If vinegar was made without throwing anything, then there are two opinions.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on wine turning into vinegar and contrasts it with the Shafi'i position, making it an inter-madhhab dispute. The Hanafi position, stated by Al-Quduri, is clear: when wine (khamr) becomes vinegar (خل), it becomes halal, whether the transformation happens spontaneously (by itself) or is induced by adding something (like salt, onion, etc.). Furthermore, actively converting wine into vinegar is not even makruh; it is permissible. The Shafi'i position differs significantly: Imam Shafi'i holds that making vinegar from wine is makruh (disliked). Regarding the resulting vinegar's permissibility, if an external substance was added to cause the transformation, the vinegar is unanimously haram (one qawl). If the transformation occurred without any addition, there are two opinions: one that it becomes halal and another that it remains haram. The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that for Hanafis, vinegar derived from wine is always halal and its production is not disliked, while Shafi'is impose restrictions based on the method of transformation.

Key Terms
خل (khall): Vinegar, specifically that produced from wine (khamr) through a chemical transformation (istihalah).
تخليل (takhallul): The process of turning wine into vinegar, either naturally or by adding a substance.
استحالہ (istihalah): Chemical transformation; the change of one substance into another, thereby altering its legal ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the ruling on wine that turns into vinegar by adding a substance?
A) It remains haram because the addition was intentional.
B) It becomes halal, and making it vinegar is not makruh.
C) It becomes halal but making it vinegar is makruh.
D) It is only halal if the transformation is spontaneous.

Key Takeaway
Istihalah (chemical transformation) is a fundamental Hanafi principle that can change the ruling of a substance from haram to halal, as seen in the case of wine turning into vinegar.

Making Vinegar from Wine: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Explains the dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'i on making vinegar from wine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on making vinegar from wine and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the legal proofs presented.

Core Idea
Making vinegar from wine is permissible because it removes the corruptive property and the resulting vinegar is beneficial.

This passage presents a classic inter-madhhab dispute concerning the legal ruling on intentionally converting wine (khamr) into vinegar. It lays out the Shafi'i objection based on the command to avoid wine and the Hanafi rebuttal rooted in hadith, the removal of the corruptive property, and Qiyas. By examining both sides, you will understand how the Hanafi school prioritizes the removal of harm and preservation of benefit.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school rules regarding takhlil. The text states that making vinegar from wine is permissible (mubah) and even preferable (awla).
2
Identify the opponent and their proof
Imam Shafi'i has two narrations; the passage focuses on the prohibiting one. His argument: takhlil involves proximity (qurb) to wine on the basis of wealth, which contradicts the command to avoid wine.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof includes: (1) Hadith 'What an excellent condiment is vinegar' — praising the product; (2) Removal of the wasf mufsid (corruptive property); (3) Analogies with naturally soured wine and tanning of hides; (4) Principle that proximity to remove corruption is permissible (like spilling).
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafis permit and prefer takhlil; Shafi'is (in the prohibitory narration) forbid it. This affects whether a Muslim can consume vinegar made from wine or handle the wine for that purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الاشربة

میں امام شافعی کے دو قول ہیں ایک یہ کہ حلال ہے اور دوسرا یہ کہ حلال نہیں ہے۔
امام شافعی کی دلیل
له ان في التخليل اقترابا من الخمر على وجه التمول والأمر بالاجتناب ينا فيه
ترجمہ: امام شافعی کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ سرکہ بنانے میں خمر سے نزدیکی ہے تمول کے طریقہ پر اور شراب سے اجتناب کا حکم اقتراب کے منافی ہے۔
تشریح یہ امام شافعی کی دلیل ہے کہ جب خمر کا سرکہ بنایا جائے گا تو یہ خمر سے تمول کے طریقہ پر قرب و نزدیکی ہے اور ہم کو خمر سے اجتناب کا حکم ملا ہے تو حکم اجتناب اور اس سے نزدیکی میں منافات ہے۔

احناف کی دلیل
ولنا قوله عليه السلام نعم الادام الخل ولان بالتخليل يزول الوصف المفسد وتثبت صفة الصلاح من حيث تسكين الصفراء وكسر الشهوة والتغذى به والاصلاح مباح وكذا الصالح للمصالح اعتبارا با المتخلل فسه والدباغ والاقتراب لاعدام الفساد فاشبه الإراقة
ترجمہ: اور ہماری دلیل نبی علیہ السلام کا فرمان ہے کہ سرکہ کیا ہی عمدہ سالن ہے اور اس لئے کہ سرکہ بنانے سے وصف مفسد زائل ہو جائے گا اور درستگی کا وصف پیدا ہو جاتا ہے یعنی صفراء کو تسکین دینا اور شہوت کو توڑنا اور ایسے ہی اس کے ذریعہ غذاء حاصل ہونا اور ایسی اصلاح مباح ہے اور ایسے ہی (حلال ہے) وہ چیز جو مصلحتوں کے اندر کام آنے کے قابل ہو جائے قیاس کرتے ہوئے خود بخود (شراب ہونے والی پر اور (ایسے ہی حلال ہو جاتا ہے) دباغت دینے سے اور نزدیکی فساد کو زائل کرنے کے لئے ہے تو یہ بہانے کے مشابہ ہو گیا۔
تشریح یہ ہماری دلیل ہے کہ حدیث میں سرکہ کو بہترین سالن ارشاد فرمایا گیا ہے پھر سرکہ بنانا حلال ہونا چاہیے کیونکہ اب وصف مفسد زائل ہو کر وصف صالح پیدا ہوتا ہے یعنی صفراء کو اس سے تسکین ملتی ہے اور شہوت ٹوٹتی ہے اور اس سے غذاء حاصل ہوتی ہے اور ایسی اصلاح درست ہے اور نیز یہ انسانی مصالح میں کام آنے کے قابل بنے گا تو جیسے جو خمر خود سرکہ بن گئی اور وہ حلال ہے تو یہ بھی حلال ہونی چاہیے اور جیسے مردار کا چمڑا دباغت دینے سے پاک ہو جاتا ہے اور اسکو دباغت دینا بلا کراہت جائز ہے اور ایسا ہی یہاں ہونا چاہیے اور جب حرام چیز سے قرب اس کے فساد کو دور کرنے کے لئے ہو تو ایسا اقتراب جائز ہے جیسے اگر شراب کو بہانا ہو تو اس سے اقتراب بالاتفاق جائز ہے۔

شراب کا سرکہ بنانے میں کوئی حرج نہیں
والتخليل اولى لما فيه من احراز مال يصير حلا لا في الثاني فيختاره من ابتلي به
ترجمہ: اور سرکہ بنانا اولی ہے بوجہ اس کے کہ اس میں ایسے مال کا احراز ہے جو آئندہ زمانہ میں حلال ہو جائے گا تو اس کو اختیار کرے وہ شخص جو اس میں مبتلا ہو۔
The Book of Beverages. Imam Shafi'i has two statements: one that it is lawful, and another that it is not lawful. Imam Shafi'i's proof: For him, in making vinegar there is proximity to wine on the basis of wealth, and the command to avoid it contradicts that. Trans: Imam Shafi'i's proof is that making vinegar involves proximity to wine on the basis of wealth, and the command to avoid wine contradicts proximity. Explanation: This is Imam Shafi'i's proof: When vinegar is made from wine, it involves proximity to wine on the basis of wealth, and we are commanded to avoid wine. So the command of avoidance and proximity to it contradict each other. The Hanafi proof: And for us is his saying (peace be upon him): 'What an excellent condiment is vinegar!' And because by making vinegar the corruptive description is removed and the description of soundness is established in terms of calming the bile, breaking lust, and providing nutrition, and such improvement is permissible. Likewise, what becomes good for benefits by analogy to what naturally becomes vinegar, and by tanning, and proximity for the sake of eliminating corruption, so it becomes like spilling. Trans: And our proof is the statement of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'What an excellent condiment is vinegar!' And because by making vinegar the corruptive description is removed and the description of soundness is established, namely calming bile, breaking lust, and obtaining nutrition through it. And such improvement is permissible. Likewise, something that becomes fit for benefits, by analogy to that which naturally becomes vinegar, and by tanning, and proximity for the purpose of eliminating corruption, so it resembles spilling. Explanation: This is our proof: The hadith praised vinegar as an excellent condiment, so making vinegar should be lawful because now the corruptive description is removed and a sound description appears: bile is soothed, lust is broken, nutrition is obtained, and such improvement is correct. Also, it becomes fit for human benefits, just as wine that turned to vinegar by itself is lawful, so this should also be lawful. And just as the hide of a carcass becomes pure by tanning, and tanning it is permissible without dislike, so it should be here. And when proximity to a forbidden thing is for the purpose of removing its corruption, such proximity is lawful, just as if one spills wine, proximity to it for spilling is unanimously lawful. There is no harm in making vinegar from wine. And making vinegar is preferable because it preserves wealth that will become lawful in the future, so the one afflicted should choose it.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the ruling on intentionally making vinegar from wine (khamr). It presents the position of Imam Shafi'i and the Hanafi response.

The passage begins by noting that Imam Shafi'i has two narrations: one permitting it and one prohibiting it. Then it states his proof: making vinegar involves drawing near to wine for the purpose of tamawul (seeking wealth from it), and the command in the Quran and Sunnah to avoid wine (ijtanibu al-khamr) prohibits such proximity. Thus, actively turning wine into vinegar contradicts the spirit of avoidance.

The Hanafi proof is presented next. It relies on a hadith: 'What an excellent condiment is vinegar' (narrated by Muslim). This indicates that vinegar itself is praiseworthy and beneficial. The Hanafi argument proceeds: making vinegar from wine removes the 'wasf mufsid' (the corruptive property of intoxication) and establishes 'sifat al-salah' (the property of soundness). This transformation brings benefits such as calming bile, curbing lust, and providing nutrition. Since the resulting improvement is permissible, the means to it is also permissible.

The Hanafi reasoning uses two analogies (qiyas):
1. Analogy with wine that turns to vinegar naturally (al-mutakhallil nafsahu): Even the strictest scholars permit naturally soured wine, as the intoxicating property is gone. Similarly, intentionally making vinegar also removes the corruption.
2. Analogy with tanning (dibaghah): The hide of an animal that died without slaughter is najis, but tanning purifies it. The act of tanning involves handling the forbidden hide, but because it serves to purify and make it useful, it is allowed. Similarly, making vinegar involves temporarily handling wine, but with the intent to remove its harm.

The passage further explains that proximity to a forbidden thing solely for the purpose of eliminating its corruption (as in spilling wine) is permissible by consensus. Making vinegar is even better because it preserves property that will become beneficial and lawful, whereas spilling is wasteful.

Thus, the Hanafi position is that making vinegar from wine is permissible and even preferable (awla) for the one afflicted with such wine, as it saves the property for halal use.

Authority weight: This is from the commentary on Al-Hidayah, which itself relies on Zahir al-Riwayah. The ruling that takhlil is permissible is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) Hanafi position, as also found in Al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
تخليل (takhleel): The process of intentionally converting wine (khamr) into vinegar.
اقتراب (iqtiraab): Proximity or drawing near to a forbidden substance; here it means handling or being close to wine.
وصف مفسد (wasf mufsid): The corruptive property, specifically the intoxicating effect that causes khamr to be forbidden.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi proof in this passage, what is the primary evidence for the permissibility of making vinegar from wine?
A) The consensus of the companions
B) The Prophet's hadith praising vinegar as an excellent condiment
C) The analogy with cleaning najasah with water
D) The principle of necessity (darurah)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits making vinegar from wine because it removes the corruptive property, as proven by hadith and analogies with natural souring and tanning.

The Hanafi Position on Making Vinegar from Wine
Inter-madhhab dispute on the permissibility of converting wine to vinegar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on takhleel al-khamr, explain the Shafi'i objection, and evaluate the Hanafi response using the hadith and qiyas.

Core Idea
Making vinegar from wine removes its corruption and transforms it into a lawful good.

Wine is fundamentally impure and intoxicating, but what if it turns into vinegar? The Hanafi and Shafi'i schools differ sharply on this question. This lesson unpacks the proofs behind each position and shows how the Hanafis use both a prophetic hadith and analogical reasoning to permit and even encourage the conversion.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the hukm level: the Hanafis rule that making vinegar from wine is mubah (permissible) and even preferable (awla). This is the mufta bihi opinion.
The text says 'والتخليل اولى' (and making vinegar is preferable).
2
Identify the Shafi'i opposition
Imam al-Shafi'i has two statements; the well-known (qawl jadid) is prohibition. His proof is that the act involves proximity to wine for gain, contradicting the command to avoid wine entirely.
Prohibition is based on the general command to avoid wine (اجتنبوا الخمر).
3
Trace the Hanafi dalil
The Hanafis rely on the hadith 'What an excellent condiment is vinegar' and a qiyas: just as wine that turns sour by itself is lawful, intentionally made vinegar is lawful because the corrupting attribute (wasf mufsid) is removed. Additional analogy: tanning makes carrion skin pure.
The hadith is from Muslim and other collections.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i objection
The Hanafis argue that proximity to remove harm (not for enjoyment) is permissible, analogous to holding wine to pour it away. The hadith praising vinegar indicates its desirability.
Iqtiraab li iddam al-fasad (proximity to eliminate corruption) is akin to irāqah (pouring away).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafis may intentionally convert wine to vinegar; Shafi'is may not (except when transformation occurs naturally).
This affects how a Muslim should deal with wine in his possession.

Explanation: This is Imam al-Shafi'i's proof: when vinegar is made from wine, it involves proximity to wine in the manner of acquiring wealth, and we have been commanded to avoid wine, so the command to avoid and proximity to it are contradictory. And because by making vinegar, the corrupting attribute is removed and the attribute of soundness is established, in terms of calming yellow bile, breaking desire, and being nourished by it, and such soundness is permissible. Also it will be of use for human interests, so just as wine that turns to vinegar by itself is lawful, this should also be lawful. Just as the skin of a carrion becomes pure through tanning and tanning is permissible without dislike, similarly here. And when proximity to a forbidden thing is to remove its corruption, such proximity is permissible, like proximity to wine to pour it away is unanimously permissible. There is no harm in making vinegar from wine. And making vinegar is preferable, because it preserves wealth that later becomes lawful; so one who is afflicted should choose this.

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the permissibility of intentionally making vinegar from wine (takhleel al-khamr). The base text first notes that Imam al-Shafi'i has two opinions: one allowing it and one forbidding it. His proof is that the act of turning wine into vinegar involves drawing close to wine for the purpose of acquiring wealth, which contradicts the command to avoid wine entirely. The Hanafi response, which is the relied-upon position, presents two proofs. First, a hadith praising vinegar as an excellent condiment implies its lawfulness; second, a detailed qiyas (analogical reasoning) showing that making vinegar removes the corrupting essence of wine (the intoxicating quality) and transforms it into something beneficial—like wine that turns to vinegar on its own, or like carrion skin purified by tanning. The Hanafis argue that proximity to wine for the sake of removing its harm is analogous to the permissibility of handling wine to pour it away. The passage concludes that making vinegar is not only permissible but preferable (awla) because it preserves wealth that becomes permissible over time. This is consistent with the Hanafi principle that istihsan (juristic preference) can override a literal analogy, especially when benefit is attained.

Key Terms
تخلیل (takhleel): The process of turning wine into vinegar intentionally.
اقتراب (iqtiraab): Drawing near or proximity, used here in the sense of handling wine for a purpose.
وصف مفسد (wasf mufsid): The corrupting attribute, i.e., the intoxicating quality of wine.

Carrying Wine to Animals: Prohibition on Active Transport
Ruling on bringing wine to animals vs. bringing animals to wine

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on carrying wine to animals and distinguish between actively moving impurities and passively allowing contact.

Core Idea
Actively carrying najis items is prohibited; bringing the animal to the najis is not.

This passage from the Hidayah commentary addresses a specific scenario involving wine and animals. The ruling distinguishes between actively transporting wine to animals and passively allowing animals to come into contact with wine. The analogy with a carcass and a dog reinforces the principle that the prohibition is on the act of moving impurities, not on incidental contact.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two cases: (1) carrying wine to animals, and (2) bringing animals to the wine. Recognize that the ruling differs based on who or what is being moved.
2
Note the ruling for each
For case 1: 'ja'iz nahi hai' (not permissible). For case 2: 'la haraj' (no harm, permissible).
3
Understand the analogy
The same distinction applies to a carcass and a dog: carrying the carcass to the dog is impermissible, but bringing the dog to the carcass is permissible. This illustrates that the principle is general: moving najis items requires necessity.
4
Extract the underlying principle
The core principle is that active transport of impurities (najis) without valid necessity is prohibited (haram), whereas allowing the animal to come to the impurity is not considered an act of transportation and thus is permissible (mubah).
This aligns with the Hanafi maxim: 'What is prohibited is the act of carrying, not the occurrence of contact.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وہ جانوروں کے پاس لے جانا جائز نہیں ہے ہاں اگر جانور کو ہا تک کر شراب پر چھوڑ دیا تو اس میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے جیسے مردار کو اٹھا کر کتے کے پاس لانا جائز نہیں ہے اور اگر کتے کو مردار کے پاس لا کر چھوڑ دیا تو اس میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے۔
It is not permissible to carry [wine] to animals; however, if the animal is brought and released upon the wine, there is no harm in it. Just as it is not permissible to carry a carcass to a dog, but if the dog is brought and released upon the carcass, there is no harm in it.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage states a ruling regarding the handling of wine in relation to animals. It is not permissible to actively transport wine to animals, such as to feed them or for other purposes, because the wine itself is najis (impure) and carrying it is an act of using it without valid necessity. However, if the animal is brought to the wine (e.g., if the wine is spilled on the ground and the animal is led to it), then there is no blame, as this is not considered active transportation of impurity. The analogy is made with a carcass (maytah): one may not carry a carcass to a dog, but if the dog is brought to the carcass, it is permissible. This reflects the Hanafi principle that the prohibition is on actively moving impurities, not on passively allowing contact.

Key Terms
حرج (haraj): Sin, blame, or legal impediment. In the passage, it means no sin or harm.
مردار (murdar): Carcass of an animal not slaughtered Islamically; ritually impure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Actively carrying wine (or any impurity) to animals is prohibited; bringing the animal to the wine is permissible.

Ruling on Adding Wine Dregs to Vinegar
Ruling on adding wine dregs to vinegar with directional condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on adding wine dregs to vinegar and identify the condition on the direction of carrying.

Core Idea
Adding wine dregs to vinegar makes them pure, but only if vinegar is carried to dregs, not dregs to vinegar.

This lesson examines a specific case of impurity transformation: when wine dregs (durdiy) are added to vinegar, they become vinegar themselves and are thus permissible. However, Hanafi fiqh imposes a subtle restriction on the manner of combining them, based on the principle of avoiding actively moving impure substances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is adding wine dregs to vinegar. The ruling is that it is permissible (لا باس به) because the dregs become vinegar.
2
Note the condition on the direction
The permissibility is conditional: it is allowed only if vinegar is carried to the dregs, not the dregs to the vinegar.
3
Understand the underlying principle
The prohibition of moving the dregs (impure) to the vinegar (pure) is to avoid actively transporting impurity to a pure substance. But if the pure substance is brought to the impure, the impurity becomes purified upon contact.
4
Compare with the analogous case
The text mentions it is similar to the previous issue of the dog and carcass, where the same principle applies.
This analogy reinforces the legal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خمر کی تلچھٹ سرکہ میں پڑنے سے سرکہ بن جائے گی
ولو القى الدردي في الخل لا باس به لانه يصير خلا لكن يباح حمل الخل اليه لا عكسه لما قلنا ترجمہ: اور اگر اس نے خمر کی تلچھٹ سرکہ میں ڈالی تو اس میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ تلچھٹ سرکہ بن جائے گی لیکن مباح سر کہ کو تلچھٹ کی طرف لے جانا نہ کہ اس کا الٹا اس دلیل کی وجہ سے جو کہ ہم بیان کر چکے ہیں۔ تشریح خمر کی تلچھٹ سرکہ میں پڑتے ہی سرکہ بن جائے گی لہذا اس میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے مگر وہاں اتنی بات ہے کہ سرکہ کو اٹھا کر خمر کی تلچھٹ کے پاس لائے نہ کہ تلچھٹ کو اٹھا کر سرکہ کے پاس لائے جیسے ابھی گذرا ہے کتے اور مردار کا مسئلہ۔
Dregs of wine fall into vinegar and will become vinegar. And if he throws the dregs into vinegar, there is no harm in that because it becomes vinegar. However, it is permissible to carry the vinegar to the dregs, not the opposite, for the reason we have mentioned. Translation: And if he throws the wine dregs into vinegar, there is no harm because the dregs will become vinegar, but it is permissible to carry vinegar to the dregs, not the opposite, due to the evidence we have stated. Explanation: When wine dregs fall into vinegar, they immediately become vinegar, so there is no harm. However, the point is that vinegar should be carried to the dregs, not the dregs to the vinegar, similar to the previous issue of the dog and carcass.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling on wine dregs (durdiy, the sediment or leftover of wine). If these dregs are added to vinegar, they turn into vinegar themselves and are thus permissible. However, a distinction is made: it is permissible to bring the vinegar to the dregs (i.e., pour vinegar over them) but not to carry the dregs to the vinegar. This follows the same logic as the previous ruling on animals and wine: one may not actively transport an impure substance (dregs) to a pure one (vinegar), but if the pure substance is brought to the impure, it is allowed because the active act of moving impurity is avoided. The analogy with the dog and carcass reinforces this.

Key Terms
دردی (durdiy): Sediment or dregs of wine; the leftover solid or thick liquid at the bottom.
خل (khall): Vinegar, considered pure and permissible in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Makruh, disliked
C) Permissible, but only if carrying vinegar to dregs
D) Permissible without restriction

Key Takeaway
Wine dregs become pure when added to vinegar, but the act of carrying the dregs to vinegar is not allowed; only vinegar may be brought to the dregs.

Using Wine for an Enema
Ruling on wine enema

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using wine for an enema and explain why the hadd penalty is not required.

Core Idea
Using wine for enema is makruh, not haram, and incurs no hadd because drinking is the specific cause for hadd.

This passage addresses a medical use of wine: administering it as an enema or into the urethra. The Hanafi school rules that this is makruh (disliked) because it involves benefiting from a prohibited substance. However, the fixed penalty (hadd) for wine consumption does not apply because the act of drinking (shurb) is absent. This distinction highlights the principle that the primary prohibition of wine is tied to oral consumption.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is using wine for an enema or urethral injection. The ruling is makruh (disliked).
2
Understand the underlying principle
The act is makruh because it is benefiting from a prohibited substance (al-muharram).
3
Analyze the consequence of hadd
Hadd is not required because the specific cause (sabab) for hadd is drinking (shurb), and that is absent here.
Hadd is a fixed punishment for consuming wine through drinking.
4
Consider practical implications
Medically necessary use of wine in enema is permissible in the sense of not incurring a sin hadd, but it remains disliked and should be avoided if alternatives exist.
This ruling does not make using wine lawful; it only limits the penalty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويكره الاحتقان وبالخمر واقطارها في الاحليل لانه الانتفاع بالمحرم ولا يجب الحد لعدم الشرب وهو السبب ولوجعل الخمر فى مرقة لاتوكل لتنجسها بها ولا حد مالم يسكر منه لانه اصابه الطبخ ويكره اكل
And it is disliked to perform an enema with wine and to apply it into the urethra because it is benefiting from a prohibited thing, and the hadd is not obligatory due to the absence of drinking, and that is the cause. And if he puts wine into soup, it is not eaten because it becomes impure, and there is no hadd unless he becomes intoxicated from it because cooking has affected it, and it is disliked to eat it.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings regarding the use of wine in non-drinking contexts. First, using wine for an enema or inserting it into the urethra (as a medical treatment) is makruh (disliked) because it involves benefiting from a forbidden substance (al-muharram). However, the hadd penalty does not apply because there is no act of drinking (shurb), which is the specific cause (sabab) for hadd. Second, if wine is put into soup (marqah), the soup becomes najis (impure) and is not to be eaten. Again, no hadd is incurred from eating the soup unless the person becomes intoxicated from it, because the wine has been affected by cooking (tabkh) which changes it. Additionally, it is disliked (yukrah) to eat such soup. These rulings reflect the Hanafi principle that the prohibition of wine relates primarily to drinking; other uses are disliked and render things impure but do not carry the full hadd penalty unless intoxication occurs.

Key Terms
حقنہ (hiqna): Enema, medical injection into the rectum.
احلیل (ihlil): Urethra, the duct for urine.
حد (hadd): Fixed penal punishment for specific crimes like drinking wine.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school not apply the hadd penalty when wine is used in an enema?
A) Because enema does not involve intoxication.
B) Because the specific cause for hadd is drinking, which is absent.
C) Because the wine is considered pure in such cases.
D) Because the act is permissible.

Key Takeaway
Using wine for enema is makruh, not haram, and no hadd is incurred due to the absence of drinking.

Using Wine in Soup
Ruling on wine in soup

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on soup containing wine and explain the conditions for the hadd penalty.

Core Idea
Wine in soup makes it impure and unlawful to eat; hadd applies only if intoxication results because cooking changes the wine.

The second part of the passage deals with adding wine to food. The Hanafi school distinguishes between the impurity (najasa) of the mixture and the criminal penalty. Adding wine to soup makes the soup najis (impure) and thus unlawful to eat. However, the hadd penalty for intoxication only applies if the person actually becomes intoxicated from it, because cooking (tabkh) affects the wine. This ruling underscores the principle that impurity and penal liability are separate matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its immediate consequence
The act is putting wine into soup. The consequence is that the soup becomes najis (impure).
2
Determine the ruling on eating the soup
The soup is not to be eaten because it is impure; it is disliked (makruh) to eat it.
The text says 'لاتوكل' (is not eaten) and 'يكره أكل' (disliked to eat).
3
Analyze the hadd penalty for the one who eats it
No hadd is incurred unless the person becomes intoxicated from eating the soup.
The reason is that cooking (tabkh) has affected the wine, changing its nature.
4
Understand the role of cooking
Cooking alters the wine, so the direct causal link to the intoxicating property may be weakened; thus hadd is not automatic.
This is a leniency from the Hanafi school.

Bringing Vinegar to Wine Dregs: Direction of Transfer and Analogy with Carrion and Dogs
Ruling on moving wine dregs to vinegar with analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on moving impure substances to pure substances, distinguishing between permissible and impermissible directions of transfer.

Core Idea
Wine dregs become pure when thrown into vinegar, but you must bring the vinegar to the dregs, not the dregs to the vinegar.

This lesson tackles a specific ruling from Hanafi fiqh: what happens when wine dregs (an impure substance) come into contact with vinegar (a pure substance)? The ruling distinguishes between two directions of movement and draws an analogy with carrion and a dog. Understanding this ruling will deepen your grasp of impurity (najasah) rules and the principle of avoiding the carrying of impurity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal ruling
First, recognize that the passage discusses two actions: (1) throwing wine dregs into vinegar, and (2) moving vinegar to the dregs. Determine the hukm for each.
The ruling is permisssible for throwing dregs into vinegar, and also permissible for bringing vinegar to dregs, but not for bringing dregs to vinegar.
2
Understand the underlying reason (illa) for the distinction
The illa (cause) is the impurity of the dregs. Carrying impurity to a pure substance is prohibited, but the opposite is permitted because the pure substance does not become impure by contact.
The analogy with carrion reinforces this: carrying carrion to a dog is not allowed, but bringing the dog to carrion is allowed.
3
Trace the qiyas (analogy) used by the author
The author analogizes the wine dregs case to the established ruling on carrion and dogs. This is a clear qiyas jaliy (explicit analogy).
Identify the asl (original case: carrion and dog), the far' (new case: wine dregs and vinegar), and the illa (carrying impurity).
4
Apply the ruling to hypothetical scenarios
Test your understanding by considering other combinations of impure and pure substances. Would the same rule apply?
e.g., milk and dung, water and dead animal. The principle remains the same.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جانوروں کے پاس لے جانا جائز نہیں ہے ہاں اگر جانور کو ہا تک کر شراب پر چھوڑ دیا تو اس میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے جیسے مردار کو اٹھا کر کتے کے پاس لانا جائز نہیں ہے اور اگر کتے کو مردار کے پاس لا کر چھوڑ دیا تو اس میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے۔ خمر کی تلچھٹ سرکہ میں پڑنے سے سرکہ بن جائے گی ولو القى الدردي في الخل لا باس به لانه يصير خلا لكن يباح حمل الخل اليه لا عكسه لما قلنا ترجمہ اور اگر اس نے خمر کی تلچھٹ سرکہ میں ڈالی تو اس میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ تلچھٹ سرکہ بن جائے گی لیکن مباح سر کہ کو تلچھٹ کی طرف لے جانا نہ کہ اس کا الٹا اس دلیل کی وجہ سے جو کہ ہم بیان کر چکے ہیں۔ تشریح خمر کی تلچھٹ سرکہ میں پڑتے ہی سرکہ بن جائے گی لہذا اس میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے مگر وہاں اتنی بات ہے کہ سرکہ کو اٹھا کر خمر کی تلچھٹ کے پاس لائے نہ کہ تلچھٹ کو اٹھا کر سرکہ کے پاس لائے جیسے ابھی گذرا ہے کتے اور مردار کا مسئلہ۔
It is not permissible to bring (animals) to wine. However, if an animal is brought to wine and left there, there is no harm. Similarly, it is not permissible to bring carrion to a dog, but if a dog is brought to carrion and left, there is no harm. Wine dregs, when thrown into vinegar, become vinegar. (Arabic:) And if one throws the dregs into vinegar, there is no harm because it becomes vinegar, but it is permissible to bring vinegar to it, not the opposite, due to what we have said. Translation: And if one throws wine dregs into vinegar, there is no harm because the dregs become vinegar. However, it is permissible to bring vinegar to the dregs, not the opposite, due to the reason we have stated. Explanation: Wine dregs, upon falling into vinegar, become vinegar, so there is no harm. But the point is that one should bring the vinegar to the wine dregs, not bring the dregs to the vinegar, just as the matter of the dog and carrion has already been mentioned.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
Passage type: Ruling with analogical reasoning. This passage presents the ruling on moving wine dregs (دردی) into vinegar, and analogizes it to the case of carrion and a dog. The principle is that one may bring the pure substance (vinegar) to the impure (dregs), but not the impure to the pure, because doing so would involve carrying an impurity. The dregs themselves, when placed into vinegar, transform into vinegar and become pure. This is similar to the example of carrion: one should not carry carrion to a dog, but if a dog is brought to carrion, it is permissible because the dog is not carrying impurity. The ruling is specific to the Hanafi school and relies on qiyas. The key point is the distinction between moving the impurity to the pure versus the pure to the impurity.

Key Terms
دردی (dirdī): The sediment or dregs left at the bottom of wine (khamr), still containing wine components.
خل (khall): Vinegar, a pure substance that can purify wine dregs when mixed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Throwing wine dregs into vinegar.
B) Carrying vinegar to the wine dregs.
C) Carrying wine dregs to the vinegar.
D) All are prohibited.

Key Takeaway
The direction of moving impurities matters: one may bring the pure to the impure, but not the impure to the pure.

Rulings on Non-Drinking Uses of Wine: Enema, Broth, and Bread
Rulings on using wine in enema, broth, and bread, with discussion of hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling (makruh/prohibited) for using wine in enema, broth, and bread, and explain why hadd is not incurred in these cases.

Core Idea
Hadd for wine requires drinking; other uses are sinful but not punishable by hadd.

Wine (khamr) is strictly prohibited, but the fixed punishment (hadd) is only triggered by drinking. This lesson examines three common scenarios where wine is used but not drunk: enema, broth, and bread. Understanding these distinctions clarifies the limits of hadd and the general prohibition of benefiting from wine.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
For each case (enema, broth, bread), determine whether the text labels it as 'makruh' or prohibits it outright. Note that all involve benefiting from a prohibited substance.
The text uses 'makruh' for enema and bread, but states broth becomes impure and cannot be eaten.
2
Locate the condition for hadd
In each case, the text explicitly states that hadd is not compulsory because drinking (shurb) is absent. Recognize that shurb is the specific cause (sabab) for hadd.
For broth, hadd is also absent unless intoxication results, because cooking alters the wine.
3
Distinguish benefit vs. drinking
Understand that any benefit from khamr is prohibited (haram), but the legal penalty is attached only to the act of drinking. This distinction prevents overapplication of hadd.
The ruling for non-drinking uses is prohibition; the hadd remains reserved for actual drinking.

Source Text

جس روٹی کا آٹا خمر سے گوندا گیا ہو اس روٹی کو کھانا مکروہ ہے کیونکہ روٹی میں شمر کے اجزاء موجود ہیں۔
And it is disliked to use wine for enema and to insert it into the penis (urethra), because it is benefiting from a prohibited thing, and the hadd is not obligatory due to the absence of drinking, and drinking is the cause. And if wine is put into broth, the broth is not eaten because it becomes impure thereby, and there is no hadd unless one becomes intoxicated from it, because cooking has affected it. Translation: And using wine for enema and inserting it into the penis is disliked, because it is benefiting from a prohibited thing. And hadd will not be obligatory due to the absence of drinking, and drinking is the cause of hadd. And if wine is put into broth, the broth will not be eaten due to its impurity caused by wine, and as long as intoxication does not occur, hadd will not be obligatory because cooking has affected it. And eating such bread, the dough of which has been kneaded with wine, is disliked, due to the presence of wine parts in it. Explanation: Every type of benefit from wine is prohibited, therefore using it for enema and the like is disliked. But if done, hadd will not be obligatory because the cause (drinking) is not present. If wine is added to broth, that broth is not permissible to eat because it becomes impure. However, if someone consumes it, hadd is not applied until intoxication, because the wine has been cooked. And bread kneaded with wine is disliked to eat because it contains parts of wine.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
Passage type: Ruling statements on various non-drinking uses of wine (khamr). The author (Imam Muhammad) states that using wine for an enema (ihtiqan) or inserting it into the urethra (ihlil) is makruh (disliked). This is because it constitutes benefiting from a prohibited substance. However, the hadd punishment is not incurred because the act of drinking (shurb) which is the cause (sabab) for hadd is absent. Similarly, if wine is added to broth (marqa), the broth becomes impure and cannot be eaten. If one consumes it, hadd is not applied unless intoxication results, because cooking alters the wine. Finally, bread made with dough kneaded with wine is makruh to eat because it retains wine components. In all these cases, the use of wine is prohibited, but the hadd penalty is conditioned on actual drinking, not other forms of consumption.

Key Terms
حقنہ (iḥtiqān): Enema, injecting liquid into the rectum.
احلیل (iḥlīl): Urethra, the passage for urine.
شرب (shurb): Drinking, the specific act that incurs hadd for wine.

Purifying Grape Juice: The Two-Thirds Boiling Principle
Defining the quantitative rule for permissible grape juice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle for purifying grape juice by calculating how much must be boiled away after skimming foam.

Core Idea
Boiled grape juice becomes halal only when two-thirds of the liquid remaining after skimming has evaporated.

In this lesson, we examine a key Hanafi principle for transforming wine into permissible grape juice. The classical texts specify that simply boiling or skimming is insufficient; a precise fraction must be removed. This rule reflects the school's concern for certainty in removing intoxicating properties. We will parse the principle and see how it applies to a concrete example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its purpose
The act is cooking (tabkh) grape juice (asir). The purpose is to remove the intoxicating quality, making the juice halal.
2
Recognize the two stages of removal
First stage: the amount lost due to boiling and skimming (zabad) is regarded as nonexistent. Second stage: from the remaining liquid, two-thirds must be boiled away.
The foam is disregarded entirely.
3
Calculate the required reduction
Apply the rule mathematically: initial volume minus skimming losses equals remaining volume. Then remove two-thirds of that remaining volume.
Example: 10 liters initial, 1 liter lost to skimming → 9 liters remain → boil away 6 liters → 3 liters halal.
4
State the legal consequence
Once the two-thirds have been removed, the remaining one-third becomes halal (permissible to drink). If less is removed, it remains haram.
This is the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في طبخ العصير
ترجمہ فصل ہے انگور کے شیرہ کو پکانے کے بیان میں
تشریح ماقبل میں یہ بات گزر چکی ہے کہ عصیر عنب کا جب تک پکانے کی وجہ سے دو ثلث ختم نہ ہو جائے تو وہ حلال نہیں ہوتا تو مصنف نے اس فصل میں پکانے کا طریقہ بیان فرمایا کہ کس طرح پکایا جائے یہاں تک کہ اس کا دو ثلث ختم ہو جائے پھر یہ بحث نہ جامع صغیر میں مذکور ہے اور نہ قدوری میں اس کو تو مصنف نے پہلے مسئلہ پر تفریح کے طور پر بیان کیا ہے اور یہ مسئلہ مبسوطات میں مذکور ہے ۔ قاعدہ اوّل دو ثلث ختم ہونے میں کون سی مقدار معتبر ہے
الاصل ان ماذهب بغليا نه بالنار وقذفه بالزبد يجعل كان لم يكن ويعتبر ذهاب ثلثى مابقى ليحل الثلث الباقي ترجمہ اصل یہ ہے کہ جو مقدار آگ کی وجہ سے جوش مارنے سے اور اس کے جھاگ مارنے سے ختم ہو جائے تو اس کو معدوم شمار کیا جائے گا اور مابقی کے دو ثلث کا ختم ہو جانا معتبر ہو گا تا کہ ثلث باقی حلال ہو جائے۔
تشریح اس فصل میں مصنف نے چند اصول بیان فرمائے ہیں یہ پہلا قاعدہ ہے جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ عصیر عنب کی جو مقدار جوش مارنے سے اور جھاگ پھینکنے سے ختم ہو جاتی ہو تو اس کو کا اعدم شمار کیا جاتا ہے اتنی مقدار کو چھوڑ کر جو باقی ہے اس کا دو ثلث جل جانا
ضروری ہے۔
مثلا دس لیٹر خمر ہے اور ایک لیٹر جوش سے اور جھاگ پھینکنے سے ختم ہو جاتی ہے تو آگے بچے نو لیٹر تو اس میں سے دونات یعنی چھ لینر کا جل جانا ضروری ہے اب جو تین لیٹر باقی بچے وہ حلال ہے۔
Chapter on cooking grape juice. Translation: Chapter on the explanation of cooking grape juice. Explanation: It has passed in the previous that grape juice does not become halal until two-thirds are removed by cooking. So the author mentions the method of cooking until its two-thirds are gone. This discussion is not in al-Jami' al-Saghir nor in al-Quduri; the author places it here as a branch. First principle: Which quantity is considered for the removal of two-thirds. (Arabic:) The original is that what disappears by its boiling due to fire and its ejection of foam is considered as if it never existed, and the removal of two-thirds of what remains is considered so that the remaining third becomes halal. Translation: The original is that the quantity that disappears by boiling due to fire and its foaming is counted as nonexistent, and the removal of two-thirds of what remains is considered so that the remaining third becomes halal. Explanation: In this chapter, the author mentions several principles. This is the first principle: The amount of grape juice that disappears by boiling and ejecting foam is considered nonexistent. Excluding that amount, two-thirds of the remaining must be burned away. For example, if there are ten liters of wine and one liter disappears due to boiling and foaming, then nine liters remain. Removing two-thirds of that, i.e., six liters, is necessary. Then the remaining three liters become halal.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
Passage type: Definition of a legal principle (qa'ida) for cooking grape juice (asir) to make it permissible. The author establishes the method: when boiling grape juice, the quantity lost through boiling and skimming (zabad) is disregarded as if it never existed. Then, from the remaining juice, two-thirds must be boiled away. Only the remaining third becomes halal. This is the relied-upon Hanafi position. The text notes that this principle is not found in al-Jami' al-Saghir or al-Quduri but appears in the Mabsoot works. An example clarifies: 10 liters of wine: if 1 liter evaporates as foam, 9 liters remain; boil away 2/3 of 9 (6 liters), leaving 3 liters halal. This is a practical rule for the transformation of wine into permissible grape juice.

Key Terms
عصیر (‏asīr): Grape juice, fresh pressed before fermentation.
غلیان (ghalyān): Boiling, the process that removes the intoxicating property.
زبد (zabad): Foam or scum that is skimmed off during boiling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) 4 liters

Key Takeaway
To make fermented grape juice halal, boil away two-thirds of the liquid that remains after disregarding foam and evaporation loss.

The Pledge as Guarantee: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i on the Nature of Rahn
Proofs for rahn as madhmun and refutation of Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi proof that rahn is madhmun and explain the thamarah al-khilaf with the Shafi'i view.

Core Idea
A pledge secures the debt; if it perishes, the debt is reduced — that is daman, not trust.

This lesson examines a key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: when a pledged item perishes in the mortgagee's possession, who bears the loss? The Hanafi school holds that the pledge is 'madhmun' (liable), meaning its destruction reduces or extinguishes the debt. The Shafi'i school considers it a trust ('amanah'), so the mortgagee must return the item or its value unless negligent. We will analyze the Hanafi proofs and their response to the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi Position
The Hanafi school rules that a pledge (rahn) is a guarantee (madhmun). If it perishes in the mortgagee's hands without negligence, the debt is reduced or extinguished.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
2
Examine the Hanafi Proofs
The text cites two hadiths and ijma. First hadith: the Prophet told the mortgagee that his right was extinguished when the horse died. Second hadith: if the value is ambiguous, it is set equal to the debt, and its destruction extinguishes the debt. Third proof: consensus of the Companions and Tabi'un.
Identify each proof and how it supports liability.
3
Identify the Shafi'i Counter-Proof
Imam al-Shafi'i relies on the hadith 'la yughlaqu al-rahn' — the pledge should not become closed (i.e., forfeited). He interprets this as meaning the mortgagee holds it as a trust.
The same hadith is interpreted differently.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Refutation
The Hanafi response reinterprets 'la yughlaqu' as prohibiting absolute ownership (ihtibas kulli), not negating liability. The mortgagee does not become full owner; surplus returns to mortgagor, deficiency is covered by him.
This aligns with the general principle that the owner bears profit and loss (li-sahibihi ghanmuhu wa 'alayhi gharmuhu).
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: Under Hanafi, if the pledge perishes, the debt is reduced/extinguished; under Shafi'i, the mortgagee must return its value unless negligent.
This affects the risk allocation in rahn contracts.

Source Text

امام شافعی کے استدلال کا جواب
والمراد بقوله عليه السلام لا يغلق الرهن على ما قالوا الاحتباس الكلى بان يصير مملوكا له كذا ذكر الكرخي عن السلف

The Messenger of Allah said: When the rahn becomes ambiguous, it will be against that for which it is a substitute. Meaning, according to the saying of the mashayikh, when the value of the rahn becomes ambiguous after its destruction. And the Companions and Tabi'un have consensus that the rahn is guarantee (madhmun). They differ on the manner of guarantee, so to hold that it is trust (amanah) opposes consensus. Explanation: In this passage, the author presents two hadiths and consensus as his proof. The first hadith: The Messenger of Allah said to the mortgagee after the horse pledged with him perished: 'Your claim is extinguished.' This is clear evidence that the rahn is guarantee. The second hadith: When the value of the rahn becomes uncertain, whether less than the debt or more or equal, the rahn will be counted equal to the debt, and with its destruction the entire debt is extinguished. The meaning of 'idha 'iya al-rahn' is that the value of the rahn is ambiguous. The third proof: Consensus of the Companions and Tabi'un that the rahn is guarantee, even if they differ on the manner of guarantee. So Imam Shafi'i's view that it is trust opposes consensus, and opposing consensus is not permissible. Response to Imam Shafi'i's argument: And the meaning of his statement 'la yughlaqu al-rahn' according to what they said is complete detention (ihtibas kulli) such that it becomes owned by him, as al-Karkhi narrated from the Salaf. Translation: And the meaning of the Prophet's statement 'la yughlaqu al-rahn' as stated by the mashayikh is complete detention, in such a way that the rahn becomes owned by the mortgagee, as al-Karkhi narrated from the Salaf. Explanation: The hadith from which Imam Shafi'i argued was meaningless; here its meaning is explained: that the rahn should not become wholly owned by the mortgagee. Al-Karkhi narrated similarly from the Salaf. So now the meaning of the hadith is that the rahn should not be closed, i.e., the mortgagee should not become the complete owner of the entire rahn. Rather, if after selling it the mortgagee's debt is paid and something remains, then its owner is the mortgagor; and if its value does not cover the debt, the mortgagor will pay it. This meaning is expressed by 'for its owner is the profit and on him lies the loss' (li-sahibihi ghanmuhu wa 'alayhi gharmuhu).
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute. The author, Sharh al-Hidayah, presents the Hanafi position that a pledge (rahn) is a guarantee (madhmun) — the mortgagor is liable for its value if it perishes — as opposed to Imam Shafi'i's view that it is a trust (amanah) with no liability. The proof includes two hadiths: (1) The incident where the Prophet told the mortgagee after the horse died that his right was extinguished, indicating the horse stood as security. (2) A hadith about ambiguity in value: if the value is uncertain, it is set equal to the debt, and destruction extinguishes the debt entirely. The third proof is consensus (ijma) of the Companions and Tabi'un, emphasizing that they agreed on the principle of liability even if they differed on its nature. The author then refutes Imam Shafi'i's argument based on the hadith 'la yughlaqu al-rahn'. The Hanafi interpretation is that this means 'do not make it completely owned' (ihtibas kulli), not that it is a trust. The correct understanding is that the rahn remains security; any surplus returns to the mortgagor, and any deficiency must be covered by him, consistent with the principle 'li-sahibihi ghanmuhu wa 'alayhi gharmuhu' (the owner takes profit and bears loss).

Key Terms
رھن (rahn): A property given as security for a debt, held by the creditor (mortgagee).
مضمون (madhmun): Liable; the rahn is a guarantee such that if it perishes, the debt is reduced/extinguished.
امانت (amanah): Trust; the mortgagee holds the pledge as a trust and is not liable for loss unless negligent.
اجماع (ijma'): Consensus of the Companions and Tabi'un on rahn being madhmun.
لا يغلق الرهن (la yughlaqu al-rahn): The pledge should not be closed/forfeited (i.e., the mortgagee does not become full owner).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Hanafi: mortgagee is liable for loss; Shafi'i: mortgagor is liable.
B) Hanafi: debt is reduced if pledge perishes; Shafi'i: mortgagee must return the pledge's value unless negligent.
C) Hanafi: pledge must be sold immediately; Shafi'i: pledge must be kept indefinitely.
D) Both schools agree that the pledge is a trust; the difference is in the right to use it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a pledge (rahn) is a guarantee (madhmun); its destruction extinguishes or reduces the debt, grounded in hadith and ijma, and the opposing hadith is interpreted to forbid absolute ownership, not liability.

The Linguistic and Legal Foundation of Rahn as Permanent Detention
Explains rahn's linguistic meaning as permanent detention and its function as a wathiqah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic meaning of rahn as permanent detention and explain how it supports the Hanafi ruling that the mortgagee has yad al-istifa' (right to possession for recovery).

Core Idea
The word rahn itself means permanent detention, so the mortgagee's possession is for recovery, not trust.

This passage sets the foundation for the Hanafi doctrine of rahn. The author first establishes that the mortgagee has the 'hand of recovery' – the right to possess and detain the pledged property. He then proves that this detention is inherent in the very word rahn, using Quranic and poetic evidence. Understanding this linguistic basis is crucial because it directly supports the Hanafi view that rahn is a guarantee (madhmun) rather than a mere trust.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal concept introduced
The author introduces 'yad al-istifa' – the mortgagee's right to possession for recovery of debt. Recognize this as a key Hanafi term.
2
Extract the linguistic root of rahn
Analyze the word 'rahn' itself: the author states it implies permanent detention (habs da'im). This is the core linguistic claim.
Linguistic analysis is foundational for deriving legal rulings in Hanafi usul.
3
Examine the Quranic and poetic evidence
The author cites Quran 74:38 ('kullu nafsin bima kasabat raheenah') and a poetic line to demonstrate that rahn connotes detention in Arabic usage.
The verse shows the linguistic meaning; the poem illustrates common usage.
4
Link linguistic meaning to legal function
The author argues that legal meanings are derived from linguistic origins. Since rahn linguistically means detention, the shari'ah ruling of rahn includes detention, supporting the mortgagee's right to hold the property.
This step connects the linguistic dalil to the legal hukm.
5
Understand the purpose (wathiqah)
The second part explains that rahn serves as a security (wathiqah) to ensure repayment by denying the mortgagor benefit and motivating him to pay.
This rational (ma'qul) justification complements the textual evidence.

Source Text

مقصود اصلی پر تمہید کی دوسری کڑی
ولان الرهن وثيقة لجانب الاستيفاء وهو ان تكون موصلة اليه وذلك ثابت بملک اليد والحبس ليقع الامن من الجحود مخافة حجود المرتهن الرهن وليكون عاجزاً عن الانتفاع به فيتسارع الى قضاء الدين لحاجته او لضجره
ترجمہ اور اس لئے کہ رہن وصولیابی کی جانب کے لئے مضبوطی ہے اور مضبوطی یہ ہے کہ وہ وصولیابی تک پہنچانے والی ہے اور یہ ( وثیقہ کا وصولیابی تک موصل ہونا ) قبضہ اور حبس کے مالک ہونے سے ہوگا تا کہ انکار سے امن واقع ہو جائے مرتہن کے انکار کر دینے کے خوف سے رہن کا اور تا کہ ہو جائے راہن مرہون سے انتفاع سے عاجز پس را ہن سبقت کرے قرض کے ادا کرنے کی جانب اپنی حاجت کی وجہ سے یا اپنی دلی تنگی کی وجہ سے ۔
Introduction to the desired objective. Because established for the mortgagee is the hand of recovery (yad al-istifa'), which is possession (mulk al-yad) and detention, because rahn signifies permanent detention. Allah said: 'Every soul is held in pledge (raheenah) for what it has earned.' And one of them said in poetry: 'I parted from you with a rahn for which there is no release on the day of farewell, so the rahn has become closed.' And the legal rulings turn upon words in accordance with their meanings. Explanation: From here, the author sets the foundation for his desired purpose and will later say that this proves that rahn is madhmun. He states that the mortgagee has the right to recover his debt through the rahn, which the author terms 'yad al-istifa'. Then he says about this yad al-istifa that it is possession and ownership, i.e., the mortgagee has the right to take possession of the rahn and detain it with him. Now the author provides evidence for this detention: the literal meaning of rahn is permanent detention, proven by the Quranic verse and poetry. The verse 'kullu nafsin bima kasabat raheenah' means every person will be detained for his deeds of disbelief. So the meaning of rahn is permanent detention. The poet says to his beloved: 'I separated from you on the day of farewell while my heart is with you as a rahn, detained with permanent detention that cannot be redeemed', meaning it has become your eternal ownership. Here also rahn is used in the sense of permanent detention. When the literal meaning of rahn is permanent detention, then this meaning will also be considered in shari'ah. In other words, legal realities do not conflict with linguistic realities. The second link of the main objective's introduction. And because rahn is a security (wathiqah) for the side of recovery, and security means being a means to it. And this (the security being a means to recovery) is established by possession (mulk al-yad) and detention, so that there is security from denial, fearing that the mortgagee might deny the rahn, and so that the mortgagor is unable to benefit from it, thus he hastens to repay the debt due to his need or his anxiety.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a definition and rationale (tamhid) setting up the ruling that rahn is madhmun. The author first establishes the concept of 'yad al-istifa' (the hand of recovery) – the mortgagee's right to possession and detention. He then proves that detention (habs) is inherent in rahn linguistically: the word 'rahn' itself implies permanent custody. Evidence includes Quran 74:38 'kullu nafsin bima kasabat raheenah' (every soul is held in pledge for its deeds) and a poetic example. The author argues that shari'ah meanings are built on linguistic origins, so rahn in law also implies detention. The second part explains the purpose: rahn is a security (wathiqah) to ensure repayment. The mortgagee's possession and the mortgagor's loss of benefit motivate the mortgagor to pay his debt. This rational analysis (istihsan or ma'qul) supports the Hanafi position that the rahn is a guarantee – the mortgagee holds it not as a trust but as security. The author connects linguistic meaning to legal function, demonstrating the coherence of the Hanafi doctrine.

Key Terms
ید الاستيفاء (yad al-istifa'): The right and possession for recovery of debt; the mortgagee's authority to take and hold the rahn to secure repayment.
حبس دائم (habs da'im): Permanent detention; the essential linguistic meaning of rahn that implies ongoing custody.
وثيقة (wathiqah): Security; rahn serves as a means to ensure repayment of debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) A gift

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi view that rahn creates liability (madhmun) is rooted in its linguistic meaning of permanent detention, which gives the mortgagee the right to possess and detain the property for debt recovery.

Liability of Excess Value in Pawn: Hanafi Majority vs. Imam Zufar
Presents Hanafi proof and response to Imam Zufar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the mortgagee's liability for the excess value of a pawn and explain the evidence and the refutation of Imam Zufar's argument.

Core Idea
The mortgagee is only liable for the debt, not the excess value, because his possession is for recovery and the excess is held only out of necessity.

In this passage, we examine a key intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the mortgagee's liability when a pawn's value exceeds the debt. Imam Zufar argued that the mortgagee guarantees the entire pawn, but the majority Hanafi position limits liability to the debt amount. The author presents the Hanafi proof from narrations and rational reasoning, then interprets a hadith cited by Imam Zufar as referring to sale, not destruction.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi majority position
Identify the rule: the mortgagee is only liable for the amount of the debt; the excess is a trust (amanah).
This is the position of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
2
Identify the opposing view
Imam Zufar holds that the mortgagee is liable for the entire pawn, including the excess value.
This is an intra-Hanafi difference.
3
Follow the Hanafi evidence
The Hanafi majority relies on a narration from 'Umar and Ibn Mas'ud and a rational proof: the mortgagee's possession is for recovery (istifa'), so guarantee is limited to the recovered amount.
The excess is pawned only out of necessity, and necessity does not extend to guarantee.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response to Imam Zufar
Imam Zufar's hadith is interpreted as referring to the case of sale, not destruction. Imam 'Ali's statement that the mortgagee is a trustee for the excess supports this reading.
The discussion is about destruction, not sale.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If the pawn is destroyed, the mortgagee compensates only the debt under the majority view, but the full value under Imam Zufar's view.
This has practical implications for pawn contracts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And our madhhab is narrated from 'Umar and 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with them), and because the mortgagee's possession is a possession for recovery, so it does not necessitate guarantee except to the extent recovered, as in actual recovery. And the excess is pawned out of necessity to prevent the separation of the principal from it, but there is no necessity regarding guarantee. Response to Imam Zufar's argument: This is the response to the hadith by which Imam Zufar argued. Its gist is that Imam Zufar was mistaken; the hadith does not mean what he understood. Rather, the meaning is that if the pawn is sold, they will transact based on the increase or decrease. And in sale, we also hold that view. Our discussion is about the destruction of the pawn, which is not intended in the hadith.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi position on the liability of the mortgagee for the excess value of a pawn beyond the debt. The author begins by stating the Hanafi evidence: a report from 'Umar and Ibn Mas'ud supports the view that the mortgagee is only liable for the amount equal to the debt. The rational proof is that the mortgagee's possession is a 'qabd al-istifa'' (possession for recovery), similar to actual recovery, where only the exact amount recovered is guaranteed. The excess is held as pawn only out of necessity because the principal cannot be separated from it, but this necessity does not extend to imposing guarantee on the excess. Then the author addresses Imam Zufar's counterargument based on a hadith. The response clarifies that the hadith refers to the case of sale, not destruction. Imam 'Ali's statement that the mortgagee is a trustee for the excess confirms that the hadith is about sale. Thus, the Hanafi position remains valid.

Key Terms
يد الاستيفاء (yad al-istifa'): A possession aimed at recovering a debt; the mortgagee's possession of the pawn is considered as recovery for the debt.
الزيادة (al-ziyadah): The excess value of the pawn over and above the debt.
التراد (al-tarad): Transaction regarding increase or decrease, typically in the context of sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

The mortgagee is only liable for the amount of the debt; the excess value of the pawn is held as a trust (amanah) and is not guaranteed.
